        gptSubPage = window.digitalData && digitalData.page && digitalData.page.subPage ? digitalData.page.subPage : "blank";

        function gptClean(gptText) {
            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adfm.202406499')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="b55dfa1bda7d4db39bfd152f29414f72">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '890381bb-1290-4346-a28a-eec423f60264'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en" style="">
         <h2 id="d167647071" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>A distinguishing feature of MAX phases and their MXene derivatives is their remarkable chemical diversity. This diversity, coupled with the 2D nature of MXenes, positions them as outstanding candidates for a wide range of electrochemical applications. Chemical disorder introduced by a solid solution can improve electrochemical behavior. Up to now, adding considerable amount of tungsten (W) in MAX phase and MXenes solid solutions, which can&nbsp;enhance electrochemical performance, proved challenging. In this study, the synthesis of M site Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub> solid solution MAX phases are reported. The 211-type (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC exhibits a disordered solid solution, whereas the 312-type (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> displays a near-ordered structure, resembling <i>o</i>-MAX, with W atoms preferentially occupying the outer planes. Solid-solution MXenes, Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, are synthesized via selective etching of high-purity MAX powder precursors containing 20%&nbsp;W. These MXenes are evaluated as sodium-ion battery anodes, with Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> showing exceptional capacity, outperforming existing multilayer MXene chemistries. This work not only demonstrates the successful integration of W in meaningful quantities into a double transition metal solid solution MAX phase, but also paves the way for the development of cost-effective MXenes containing W. Such advancements significantly widen their application spectrum by fine-tuning their physical, electronic, mechanical, electrochemical, and catalytic properties.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406499-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>MAX phases are a diverse family of atomically-layered compounds that follow the formula M<sub>n+1</sub>AX<sub>n</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0001_R_d167646962e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Trends Chem.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0002_R_d167646962e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Where n is a positive integer (typically 1 to 4), M represents an early transition metal, A represents a group-A element, typically from groups 13 and 14 of the periodic table, and X is either carbon and/or nitrogen. Owing to their layered structure and mixture of covalent, ionic, and metallic bonds, these materials exhibit a distinctive combination of physical, mechanical, and electronic properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0003_R_d167646962e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Am. Ceram. Soc. Bull.">3</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0006_R_d167646962e550" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - J. Eur. Ceram. Soc.">6</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, MAX phases serve as a precursor for creating MXenes, a class of 2D carbides and nitrides that are synthesized by selective etching of the A layer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0007_R_d167646962e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Nat. Synth.">7</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0008_R_d167646962e556" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Nat. Protoc.">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes are the most diverse family of 2D materials, as they can be directly derived from their parent MAX phases while preserving their inherent carbide structure and chemical composition.</p>
         
         <p>MAX phases exhibit high chemical versatility and numerous quaternary (or beyond) solid solutions have been synthesized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0001_R_d167646962e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Trends Chem.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0002_R_d167646962e564" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MAX phases are able to form various solid solutions in either of the M, A, or X constituents, possibly having multiple components in these respective sites. The most widespread solid solutions are double transition metal MAX phases (M′<sub>1-x</sub>M″<sub>x</sub>)<sub>n</sub>AX<sub>n-1</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0009_R_d167646962e578" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - MRS Bull.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> These types of MAX phase solid solutions can be either disordered or ordered. Where ordering can be out-of-plane or in-plane, termed <i>o</i>-MAX and <i>i</i>-MAX, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0001_R_d167646962e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Trends Chem.">1</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0010_R_d167646962e588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10">10</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>A prominent feature of solid solution MAX phases is that they can be used as precursors to synthesize MXenes that inherit their M′<sub>1-x</sub>M″<sub>x</sub> complex structures and tunable properties. Therefore, compelling a continuous strive toward discovery of new MAX phases structures and compositions.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0011_R_d167646962e599" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Science">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In turn, these can be used to synthesize novel MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0012_R_d167646962e604" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As attested via the development of complex solid solutions and even high-entropy MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0013_R_d167646962e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - ACS Nano">13</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0014_R_d167646962e610" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Mater.">14</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Modifying solid solution MAX and MXene by incorporating different elements enables to enhance or tune their mechanical, thermal, electronic, optical, and electrochemical properties.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0012_R_d167646962e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0015_R_d167646962e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Scr. Mater.">15</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0017_R_d167646962e615" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For instance, the chemical diversity and/or disorder of solid solution MAX phases and MXenes can make them promising catalyst<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0018_R_d167646962e621" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - J. Mater. Chem. A">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or electrode<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0019_R_d167646962e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - JPhys Energy">19</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0021_R_d167646962e626" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Renew. Sustainable Energy Rev.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> materials. So far, over 80 (M′<sub>1-x</sub>M″<sub>x</sub>)<sub>n+1</sub>AX<sub>n</sub> disordered M site solid solutions have been synthesized.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0002_R_d167646962e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Mater. Today">2</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Among their many potential uses, MXenes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0022_R_d167646962e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">22</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0025_R_d167646962e647" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - InfoMat">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and MXene-based composites<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0026_R_d167646962e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - ChemElectroChem">26</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0029_R_d167646962e652" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Chem. Eng. J.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> show promise as high-performance anode materials in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs), which are persistently sought after as a promising cost-effective alternative to lithium-ion batteries.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0030_R_d167646962e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">30</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0031_R_d167646962e657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, tuning MXene chemistry via M site solid solutions is a promising strategy to improve electrochemical properties such as capacity and cyclic stability of battery electrodes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0017_R_d167646962e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nano Energy">17</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0020_R_d167646962e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - J. Mater. Chem. A">20</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0032_R_d167646962e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - J. Alloys Compd.">32</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0033_R_d167646962e663" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Tungsten (W) is a heavy transition metal element that offers a high valence state and strong bonding with oxygen and carbon. W-based materials are known for their electrochemical and catalytic performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0034_R_d167646962e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">34</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0036_R_d167646962e671" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - ChemElectroChem">36</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Incorporating W into the structure of Ti-based MAX and MXenes can enable tuning of their physical and electronic properties. Possibly yielding compounds with high thermal and electrical conductivities, higher strength at elevated temperature, lower thermal expansion, and resistance to thermal shock.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0037_R_d167646962e676" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Moreover, W-based MXenes could be promising topological insulators,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0038_R_d167646962e681" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Phys. Rev. B – Condens. Matter Mater. Phys.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> gas sensors,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0039_R_d167646962e687" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Appl. Surf. Sci.">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> catalysts,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0037_R_d167646962e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0040_R_d167646962e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Sci. Rep.">40</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0042_R_d167646962e692" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - FlatChem">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and electrodes energy storage and conversion.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0035_R_d167646962e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Tungsten">35</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0043_R_d167646962e698" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For example, the implementation of other 2D W-based materials like WS<sub>2</sub> and WSe<sub>2</sub> dichalcogenides as anodes in SIBs has also been considered.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0044_R_d167646962e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - J. Energy Chem.">44</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0045_R_d167646962e707" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Adv. Mater.">45</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>To date, there are very few examples of MAX phases and even less MXenes that contain tungsten. Meshkian et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0037_R_d167646962e714" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized (W<sub>2/3</sub>Sc<sub>1/3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (W<sub>2/3</sub>Y<sub>1/3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC <i>i</i>-MAX phases and used them as precursors to make W<sub>1.33</sub>C MXenes with vacancy ordering, which exhibited superior catalytic performance. Yang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0046_R_d167646962e737" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Carbon">46</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized a variety of solid solution <i>i</i>-MAX phases comprising (W<sub>2/3</sub>R<sub>1/3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC with R being a lanthanide (Gd, Tb, Dy, Ho, Er, Tm, and Lu). Similarly, Chen et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0047_R_d167646962e751" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Scr. Mater.">47</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized (W<sub>1/3</sub>Mo<sub>1/3</sub>R<sub>1/3</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC with R being a lanthanide (Gd, Tb, Dy, Ho, Er, and Y). Notably all these W-containing MAX phases have a 211 structure and do not contain any Ti. Instead, they contained costly rare earth elements. A few other examples with limited amounts of W have been reported. Cai et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0048_R_d167646962e765" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - Ceram. Int.">48</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized Nb<sub>3.9</sub>W<sub>0.1</sub>AlC<sub>3</sub> but only 2.5% of Nb was substituted by tungsten. Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0049_R_d167646962e777" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - J. Mater. Sci. Mater. Electron.">49</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>SiC<sub>2</sub> but reported that only 5&nbsp;mol.% W could be incorporated into the MAX phase. Furthermore, although Si-based MAX phases serve as MXene precursors,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0050_R_d167646962e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">50</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> it is much more convenient to have Al-based MAX phases that are easier to selectively etch. Anasori et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0051_R_d167646962e796" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Inorg. Chem.">51</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> synthesized W-based nanolaminated carbides (W,Ti)<sub>4</sub>C<sub>4-x</sub>, which cannot serve as a precursor for MXenes. Up to now, to the best of our knowledge,&nbsp;disordered transition metal quaternary MAX phases that contain a considerable amount of tungsten as a second M″ element have not been realized.</p>
         
         <p>In the present study we investigated the synthesis of double transition metal Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub> solid solutions with 10–40% W substituting Ti in (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> structures. The phases obtained for the various compositions were analyzed and focus was given to the characterization of Ti–20%W MAX phases and their corresponding MXenes. Finally, the performance of W-containing solid solution multilayered MXenes as SIB anodes was assessed.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406499-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0030-title">2.1 Effect of Varying Tungsten Content on the Synthesis Products</h3>
            
            <p>The phase compositions following synthesis of (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> with varying W content (x = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4) were determined by X-ray diffraction (XRD) (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0001">1</a>). The phase fractions weight percentages were determined by Rietveld refinement and are detailed in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202406499-tbl-0001">1</a>. For an addition of 10%&nbsp;W, nearly pure Ti<sub>1.8</sub>W<sub>0.2</sub>AlC (211 MAX) and pure Ti<sub>2.7</sub>W<sub>0.3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (312 MAX) were obtained, with a small trace of (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (1.6&nbsp;wt.%) in the 211 phase and TiC (1.2&nbsp;wt.%) in the 312 phase. Similarly, for the addition of 20%&nbsp;W nearly pure Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC or Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> were obtained, with only a negligible trace of 312 MAX (2.0&nbsp;wt.%) or W (0.7&nbsp;wt.%), respectively. In stark contrast to the high purity of the MAX phases obtained at 10–20% W, adding 30% or 40%&nbsp;W led to significant amounts of various other phases, including different MAX phases, metal carbides, intermetallic and residual metals. Additionally, there are trace amounts of other phases that did not have sufficient intensity to be properly identified.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/ac46dc2a-230e-4339-900a-341645acf772/adfm202406499-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/ac46dc2a-230e-4339-900a-341645acf772/adfm202406499-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/bf55415f-3660-4dc2-bc1f-323a2df02d09/adfm202406499-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/ac46dc2a-230e-4339-900a-341645acf772/adfm202406499-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">XRD analysis. a) XRD patterns of synthesized powders aimed at solid solution MAX phases (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> with varying W content (x = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, 0.4). b) Zoom-in on the high-intensity peaks around 39–40° 2θ, indicating a shrinkage in the lattice parameter. Crystal data on the detected phases can be found in the (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S13</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S9</a>, Supporting Information).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>For the targeted 211 (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC stoichiometry, addition of 30%&nbsp;W led to formation of a 312 MAX phase (Ti<sub>2.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, 26.8&nbsp;wt.%) alongside the desired 211 MAX phase (Ti<sub>1.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC, 59.6&nbsp;wt.%). In addition, new rhombohedral (<i>R3m</i>) intermetallic phases comprising Al<sub>x</sub>Ti<sub>y</sub>W<sub>z</sub> were detected (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a>, Supporting Information). These intermetallics are structurally analogous to the rhombohedral distorted γ-phase like Au<sub>5</sub>Zn<sub>8</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0052" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0052_R_d167646962e971" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 52 - Inorg. Chem.">52</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and their composition was estimated by energy dispersive spectroscopy (EDS). Specifically for the targeted 211 with 30%&nbsp;W addition, the phase Al<sub>15.4</sub>Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>8.2</sub> (9.9&nbsp;wt.%) was found. Furthermore, some metallic W (3.7&nbsp;wt.%) was detected. Addition of 40%&nbsp;W resulted in a complete absence of a 211 MAX phase, instead showcasing a 312 MAX phase (Ti<sub>2.1</sub>W<sub>0.9</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, 67.0&nbsp;wt.%) alongside abundant Al<sub>17.6</sub>Ti<sub>2.5</sub>W<sub>5.8</sub> (16.2&nbsp;wt.%) and metallic W (16.8&nbsp;wt.%). Naturally, all the synthesized 211 MAX phase solid solutions exhibit a single unique Wycoff position (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a>, Supporting Information) that is shared between the W and Ti atoms (with partial occupancies). Leading to an equimolar disorder throughout the structure, with the W:Ti distribution changing in a linear fashion, that is, W<sub>0.1</sub>:Ti<sub>0.9</sub>, W<sub>0.2</sub>:Ti<sub>0.8</sub>, and W<sub>0.3</sub>:Ti<sub>0.7</sub> for the 211 MAX (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC (x = 0.1, 0.2, 0.3) phases, respectively.</p>
            
            <p>In the case of the targeted 312 (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> stoichiometry, it became increasingly apparent that the MAX phase tends&nbsp;to exhibit out-of-plane ordering with rising W content (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). In contrast to the 211 phases, the unit cells of the 312 phases contain two unique Wycoff positions. One relating to the outer planes – <i>4f</i>, with M in closer proximity to A, and the other relating to the inner “sandwiched” plane of M – <i>2a</i>. Examination of these unique positions makes it clear that the substitution of Ti by W primarily occurs at the outer planes, as indicated by the refinement of partial occupancies (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5–S8</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a>, Supporting Information). This seems to be in line with the first principal calculations conducted by Dahlquist and Rosen.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0053_R_d167646962e1044" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Suggesting that under the synthesis conditions used herein, T = 1723 K, the observed disorder in the <i>2a</i> and <i>4f</i> positions results in Δ<i>H</i> &lt; 0 for solid solutions containing W in a TiM<sub>2</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> system. It is worth noting that other group VI transition elements (i.e., Cr and Mo) readily form <i>o</i>-MAX structures, as is the case of (Cr<sub>0.5</sub>V<sub>0.5</sub>)<sub>n+1</sub>AlC<sub>n</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0054" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0054_R_d167646962e1070" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 54 - Mater. Res. Lett.">54</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or (Ta<sub>1-x</sub>Ti<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0055" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0055_R_d167646962e1084" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 55 - RSC Adv.">55</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, in these cases, the MAX phase solid solutions exhibit ordering tendencies toward the <i>4f</i> position alone. This well-defined preferential exchange at the outer planes is perceivable by the gradual change in the basal peak intensity ratios for the solid solution (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> phases compared to Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, whereby the intensities of the (0002) and (0004) reflections diminish and grow, at 2θ = 8° and 2θ = 17°, respectively, as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0001">1a</a> for the 312 MAX phases.</p>
            
            <p>Comprehensive lists of the synthesis targeted compositions, resulting phases by weight percentage, and Rietveld analysis of the X-ray diffraction (XRD) spectra are detailed in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1–S8</a> and Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2–S9</a> (Supporting Information). The crystal structure diagrams of the solid solution MAX and <i>o</i>-MAX phases are shown in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9–S12</a> (Supporting Information). The analysis confirms the hexagonal space group P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc for both the 211 and 312 MAX phase solid solutions, with some important distinctions regarding internal composition.</p>

         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0040-title">2.2 Crystallographic Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Analysis of the refinement results shows that for the 10%&nbsp;W addition the <i>4f</i> position exhibits a W<sub>0.14</sub>:Ti<sub>0.86</sub> distribution, while the <i>2a</i> inner W/Ti position results in a W<sub>0.02</sub>:Ti<sub>0.98</sub> distribution. Progressing in a linear fashion, the 20%&nbsp;W addition exhibits a W<sub>0.28</sub>:Ti<sub>0.72</sub> and W<sub>0.04</sub>:Ti<sub>0.96</sub> distribution in the <i>4f</i> and <i>2a</i> positions, respectively. However, addition of 30%&nbsp;W led to a mixture of products, with two predominant ordered MAX phases: 1) a 413 MAX phase W<sub>2</sub>T<sub>2</sub>AlC<sub>3</sub> (48.1&nbsp;wt.%) analogous to M″<sub>2</sub>M′<sub>2</sub>AlC<sub>3</sub><sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0056" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0056_R_d167646962e1525" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 56 - J. Appl. Phys.">56</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <i>o</i>-MAX structures (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a>, Supporting Information). Containing two distinct Wycoff positions, one relating to the outer planes, <i>4e</i>, with a W<sub>0.8</sub>:Ti<sub>0.2</sub> distribution, and the second relating to the inner planes, <i>4f</i>, with a W<sub>0.2</sub>:Ti<sub>0.8</sub> distribution. 2) The target 312 MAX phase, Ti<sub>2.1</sub>W<sub>0.9</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (42.4&nbsp;wt.%). However, with a W:Ti distribution that deviated from the linear trend observed for the 10–20% W additions (similar to the 211 MAX phases). In this case, it encompassed a much more significant exchange of the Ti by W in the inner layer, resulting in a W<sub>0.14</sub>:Ti<sub>0.86</sub> and W<sub>0.38</sub>:Ti<sub>0.62</sub> distribution in the <i>2a</i> and <i>4f</i> positions respectively. Had the exchange been linear with previous stoichiometries the distribution in the <i>2a</i> position would be W<sub>0.06</sub>:Ti<sub>0.94</sub>. In addition, small amounts of an intermetallic Ti<sub>17.6</sub>W<sub>8.4</sub> (5.3&nbsp;wt.%) phase and W metal (1.4&nbsp;wt.%) and TiC (2.8&nbsp;wt.%) were observed. Finally, the most diverse structural array was obtained by adding 40%&nbsp;W to the targeted 312 stoichiometry. In this case, the main phase obtained was W<sub>2</sub>TiAlC<sub>2</sub> (60.4&nbsp;wt.%), which is analogous to other <i>o</i>-MAX structures of the M″<sub>2</sub>M'AlC<sub>2</sub> family.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0057" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0057_R_d167646962e1590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 57 - Acta Mater.">57</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It exhibited W:Ti distributions of W<sub>0.2</sub>:Ti<sub>0.8</sub> and W<sub>0.9</sub>:Ti<sub>0.1</sub> in the <i>2a</i> and <i>4f</i> positions, respectively. The remaining phases observed in the mixture were TiC (24.3&nbsp;wt.%), Ti<sub>1.5</sub>W<sub>0.5</sub>C (7.6&nbsp;wt.%), <i>β</i>-Ti (5.5&nbsp;wt.%)—a high-temperature Ti polymorph, and W (2.2&nbsp;wt.%).</p>
            
            <p>As noted, smaller fractions of W are localized in the central layer in the obtained 312 MAX solid solutions. A feature that indicates W atoms behave more similarly to Cr rather than Mo, which typically exists only in the outer <i>o</i>-MAX layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0058" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0058_R_d167646962e1619" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 58 - Nat. Nanotechnol.">58</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is anticipated that the overall lattice will shrink since the atomic radius of W (135 pm) is slightly smaller than that of Ti (140 pm).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0059" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0059_R_d167646962e1624" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 59 - J. Phys. Chem. A">59</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is plainly observed by the shift to higher 2θ° angles with W content in both types of MAX phases, as shown for the high intensity peaks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0001">1b</a>). In both (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> the <i>a</i> lattice parameter decreased linearly with an increasing amount of W (0–30%), from 3.056 to 3.025 Å and 3.073 to 3.043 Å in the 211 and 312 phases, respectively (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6</a>). Moreover, it further shrunk down to 3.001 Å in the 312 W<sub>2</sub>TiAlC <i>o</i>-MAX phase (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information), seemingly obeying Vegard's law within the Ti/W carbide region. However, in contrast to the <i>a</i> lattice parameter, the <i>c</i> lattice parameter displayed an abnormal behavior, particularly pronounced in the 312-type structure (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0002">2b</a>), as compared to the parent Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> phase. Initially, the <i>c</i> parameter decreases from 18.581 to 18.461 Å at 10%&nbsp;W for Ti<sub>2.7</sub>W<sub>0.3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, followed by a further decrease to 18.445 Å at 20%&nbsp;W for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>. Subsequently, there was an increase to 18.497 Å at 30%&nbsp;W for Ti<sub>2.1</sub>W<sub>0.9</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, and finally to <i>c</i> = 18.689 Å for the W<sub>2</sub>TiAlC <i>o-</i>MAX phase, obtained at 40%&nbsp;W, surpassing even the <i>c</i>/<i>a</i> ratio of the parent Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>-type structure. This behavior contrasts with other reports on M site solid solutions, where both <i>a</i> and <i>c</i> concurrently decrease or increase depending on the substituting element.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0015_R_d167646962e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Scr. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0060" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0060_R_d167646962e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 60 - J. Solid State Chem.">60</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0061" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0061_R_d167646962e1716" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 61 - Mater. Res. Lett.">61</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The abrupt increase in <i>c</i> when increasing the W content from 20 to 30% is attributed to the non-linear exchange of Ti atoms by W in the inner M layer at the <i>2a</i> position.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/dce4e3b5-cbfe-432b-8033-ff8296e39e91/adfm202406499-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/dce4e3b5-cbfe-432b-8033-ff8296e39e91/adfm202406499-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ff44c23c-975a-425c-8770-3c473db61cb2/adfm202406499-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/dce4e3b5-cbfe-432b-8033-ff8296e39e91/adfm202406499-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The change in (a) <i>a</i> and b) <i>c</i> lattice parameters, c) <i>c</i>/<i>a</i> ratios, and d) unit cell volume of (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> with addition of between 0 to 30%&nbsp;W (x = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3).</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Thus, the inclination toward out-of-plane ordering for the 312 structure alters the crystal unit cell so that the <i>c</i>/<i>a</i> ratio changes irregularly (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0002">2c</a>) when accommodating W in a non-linear fashion in the two distinct Wycoff positions. In contrast, as the 211 structure is limited to exchange of Ti by W in a single Wycoff position, the <i>c</i>/<i>a</i> ratio increases linearly. Notwithstanding, when considering the slightly smaller atomic radius of W compared to that of Ti, arising from the lanthanide contraction phenomenon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0062" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0062_R_d167646962e1783" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 62 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">62</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> the overall unit cell volume of the phases in both the cases follows a definitive downward trend (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0002">2d</a>). Decreasing from 110.18 to 107.98 Å3 and 151.96 to 148.35 Å3 for the 211 and 312 MAX phases, respectively. As expected, the volume of the W<sub>2</sub>TiAlC <i>o</i>-MAX phase decreased even further to 145.81 Å3.</p>
            
            <p>A possible fundamental insight into the MAX structure change can be gained when considering the bond lengths of the <i>4f</i> and <i>2a</i> positions containing W/Ti in relation to the C and Al atomic layers situated between them. The atomic positions of the <i>4f</i> W/Ti and C, also having a <i>4f</i> position (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0003">3a</a>), were refined. During refinement the <i>4f</i> positions that are located within the unit cell could move freely along the z direction (coordinates [1/3, 2/3, z] for W/Ti, [2/3, 1/3, z] for C). Showing a linear decrease in the <i>4f</i> W/Ti–C average bond length (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0003">3b</a>) with increased content of W, from 214 to 208 pm. While the <i>2a</i> position, located on the edge of the unit cell (coordinates 0, 0, 0), shows an increase in the average <i>2a</i> W/Ti–C bond length with a quadratic dependency (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0003">3c</a>), from 214 to 225 pm. The average <i>4f</i> W/Ti–Al bond length also features a linear decrease, from 288 to 277 pm (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0003">3d</a>), as do the Al─Al bond lengths, from 307 to 304 pm, which are tied to the linear decrease in the <i>a</i> unit cell parameter.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c2d67d5d-1c58-4669-9abe-8144c616e99e/adfm202406499-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/c2d67d5d-1c58-4669-9abe-8144c616e99e/adfm202406499-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5ef07dac-726c-4e5d-aa06-99ef25973358/adfm202406499-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c2d67d5d-1c58-4669-9abe-8144c616e99e/adfm202406499-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Changes in bond lengths for the (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> phases with 0–30% W added. a) Wycoff positions of the atoms comprising the asymmetric unit within the unit cell. b) Linear decrease in the <i>4f</i> W/Ti-C bond length, c) quadratic increase in the <i>2a</i> W/Ti-C bond length, and d) linear decrease in the <i>4f</i> W/Ti-Al bond length.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The higher electronegativity associated with the W atoms<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0053" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0053_R_d167646962e1874" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 53 - Phys. Chem. Chem. Phys.">53</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> necessitates compensation in the chemical composition of the disordered <i>4f</i> and <i>2a</i> sites in Ti<sub>2.1</sub>W<sub>0.9</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>. This leads to the large non-linear increase in W content at the <i>2a</i> position for the addition of 30%&nbsp;W. As the bond length of W/Ti-C in this position, 225 pm, surpasses even that of pure tungsten carbide, 220 pm.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0063" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0063_R_d167646962e1892" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 63 - J. Phys. Chem. Solids">63</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In turn, this causes a considerable weakening of the inner layer W/Ti─C bond and facilitating the formation of the various phases observed in the solid mixture. It is worth noting that for the <i>o</i>-MAX phase, W<sub>2</sub>TiAlC<sub>2</sub>, the bond length between the <i>2a</i> and <i>4f</i> positions of W/Ti and C is relaxed to bond lengths of 214 and 215 pm, respectively, which is closer to that of the parent Ti<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>. This relaxation is consistent with a local minimum of energy observed for another group VI substituted 312 MAX phase, Cr<sub>2</sub>TiAlC<sub>2</sub>, investigated by Burr et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0064" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0064_R_d167646962e1917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 64 - Mater. Res. Lett.">64</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0050-title">2.3 MAX Phases Microstructure Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> solid solution MAX phases with 20%&nbsp;W were selected as the MXene precursors. A choice based on the synthesis products having high purity, 98 and 97% for the 211 and 312, respectively, while containing a significant amount of W. The analyzed crystal structure of the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC (211) and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (312) phases are shown in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0004">4a,b</a>. As mentioned above, the crystalline lattice of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC exhibits disorder within a single Wycoff position with a mixture ratio of Ti<sub>0.8</sub>:W<sub>0.2</sub>, while for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, the crystalline lattice exhibits disorder within two distinct Wycoff positions, <i>2a</i> and <i>4f</i>, showing mixtures of Ti<sub>0.96</sub>:W<sub>0.04</sub> and Ti<sub>0.72</sub>:W<sub>0.28</sub>, respectively (<b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adfm202406499-tbl-0002">2</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/6eb16fad-a884-463d-a090-b5d3a7472106/adfm202406499-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/6eb16fad-a884-463d-a090-b5d3a7472106/adfm202406499-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f05dd64a-df59-4e3f-8806-3fdccbf108ce/adfm202406499-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/6eb16fad-a884-463d-a090-b5d3a7472106/adfm202406499-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

            <p>Examination of the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> solid solution MAX phases microstructure was conducted (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5</a>). The powder particles, studied by high-resolution scanning electron microscopy (HRSEM), exhibited a typical MAX phase faceted morphology with obvious layers and kinks (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5a,d</a>). The distinct atomic layering was observed by high-resolution scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) from the viewing direction of [110] and [010] zone axis in the case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5b</a>) and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5e</a>), respectively. For the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, there is a noticeable difference with the high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) contrast between the inner and outer layers of the Ti-W carbide. As shown clearly by the overlayed integrated HAADF intensity line profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5e</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14</a>, Supporting Information). The higher intensity in the outer layers reflects the preference of W atoms to occupy the <i>4f</i> position at the outer layers. Depletion of Ti and enrichment of W in the outer layers was confirmed by EDS, showing correlative periodic intensities of Ti and W (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5f</a>). This illustrates the inclination of Ti-W solid solutions toward forming an <i>o</i>-MAX structure. Further evidence of this preference is seen in the selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0005">5e</a> insert), which corresponds with the XRD observations&nbsp;(Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0001">1a</a>).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/87b521ce-8565-4662-b62d-0805ed290b32/adfm202406499-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/87b521ce-8565-4662-b62d-0805ed290b32/adfm202406499-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/44fa3237-60d8-460b-9711-0ca1a3f08b06/adfm202406499-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/87b521ce-8565-4662-b62d-0805ed290b32/adfm202406499-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructural and atomic scale characterization of Ti–20%W solid solution MAX phases a–c) 211 structure Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and d–f) 312 structure Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC. a,d) HRSEM of MAX phase particles. b,c) high-resolution STEM showing the atomic structure viewed along zone axis b) [110] and e) [010] of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC, respectively.                   </figcaption>
