            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0060-title">2.4 MXene Synthesis and Microstructural Characterization</h3>
            
            <p>Synthesis of MXenes from the Ti–20%W MAX phase solid solution precursors could not be realized by usual methods used for pure Ti-based MAX phases. More aggressive etching was required, and intercalation was not achievable using common intercalants like LiF/LiCl. Both types of MXenes, Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, required relatively longer etch times using HCl/5%HF and HCl/10%HF, respectively, and subsequent&nbsp;intercalation by TMAOH. The use of organic bases for intercalation makes it easier to exfoliate the MXene and alters the surface chemistry replacing ─F terminations with ─O and ─OH.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0065" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0065_R_d167646962e2363" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 65 - Dalt. Trans.">65</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Other reports also show that aggressive etching/intercalation approaches are required for obtaining solid solution<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0012_R_d167646962e2368" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> or Mo-based MXenes,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0066" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0066_R_d167646962e2374" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 66 - Chem. Mater.">66</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> indicating a modified chemical response of the parent solid solution MAX phase related to differences in the chemical bonding.</p>
            
            <p>The microstructural features of both kinds of obtained MXenes were examined (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6</a>). The etched multilayer MXenes of both kinds had typical morphology (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6a,e</a>). Their respective 2D atomically layered carbide structure was consistent with that of the MAX phase precursors (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6b,f</a>). The Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> displayed the same <i>o</i>-MAX inclined ordering, evident by the higher intensity of the outer layers when imaged by high-resolution STEM HAADF (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S15</a>, Supporting Information). Slight oxidation in the form of very fine nanoparticles can be observed. The single flake Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> appears to be more oxidized compared to the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, corroborated later by the X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) results. High-resolution STEM imaging of single flakes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6d,h</a>) shows the presence of heavy W atoms appearing very bright in HAADF contrast. In both kinds of MXenes a disordered distribution of the W is observed when viewed from the [001] basal plane direction. Lower magnification micrographs showing the homogenous distribution of the W atoms are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a> (Supporting Information). Both types of MXenes are also slightly perforated (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6c,g</a>) and contain vacancies (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0006">6d,h</a>), which is probably a result of over-etching.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0067" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0067_R_d167646962e2435" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 67 - ACS Nano">67</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The presence of oxidation and perforations warrants further optimization of the synthesis process of these solid solution MXenes. Nevertheless, it should be emphasized that herein we provide an alternative way to obtain W-containing MXenes, albeit in a solid solution with Ti, without requiring to etch away costly rare-earth elements in the MAX phase precursors.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0037_R_d167646962e2441" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Adv. Mater.">37</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/570be486-a76f-41e4-8e13-24ea58b9e967/adfm202406499-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/570be486-a76f-41e4-8e13-24ea58b9e967/adfm202406499-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5e348514-aad7-4789-b040-8312096e7892/adfm202406499-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/570be486-a76f-41e4-8e13-24ea58b9e967/adfm202406499-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Microstructural and atomic scale characterization of solid-solution MXenes a–d) Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, e–h) Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, a,e) HRSEM micrographs using a STEM detector and b,f) TEM HAADF high-resolution STEM of multilayered MXenes. c,g) HRSEM micrographs using a STEM detector and d,h) TEM HAADF high-resolution STEM of single flake MXenes as viewed from the [001] direction. The corresponding SAED patterns are inserted in (d,h), respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0070">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0070-title">2.5 MXene Optical, Structural, and Chemical Properties</h3>
            
            <p>The solid solution Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> MXenes exhibit altered optical properties with respect to their pure Ti<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> counterparts, as seen in their colloid absorption spectrum and the color of colloidal solution and dry films (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7a,b</a>). Compared to regular Ti-based MXenes the Ti–20%W solid solutions colloids exhibit a noticeable blueshift in their surface plasmon resonance absorption peaks.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0068_R_d167646962e2522" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Opt. Mater.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> absorbance peak shifts to ≈565&nbsp;nm compared to ≈582&nbsp;nm for Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> exhibits absorption peaks at ≈385 and ≈635&nbsp;nm compared to ≈406 and ≈900&nbsp;nm observed for Ti<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>. Note that the blueshift is very substantial in the NIR absorption peak shifting into visible wavelengths for the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>. When comparing the absorbance of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> to other M′<sub>1-x</sub>M″<sub>x</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> transition metal solid-solution systems, comprising Ti with 20% Nb or V, the effect of W shows closer resemblance to that of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>Nb<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0012_R_d167646962e2580" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The color of the different colloids was not very different, with the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> being more greenish and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> more brownish (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7a</a> insert). However, there was a noticeable difference in the color of their corresponding free-standing films (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7b</a> insert). As these colors are highly sensitive to the structure and composition of the MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0068" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0068_R_d167646962e2607" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Opt. Mater.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/f30b0295-8b1f-4ef6-8975-307d1bed7e63/adfm202406499-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/f30b0295-8b1f-4ef6-8975-307d1bed7e63/adfm202406499-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/f65c68df-d466-402c-b382-1a37179acb06/adfm202406499-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/f30b0295-8b1f-4ef6-8975-307d1bed7e63/adfm202406499-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 7<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0007&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Spectroscopic characterization of solid solution Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> MXenes. a) UV–vis–NIR spectra absorption spectra compared to literature data of regular Ti<sub>3</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0068" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0068_R_d167646962e2657" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 68 - Adv. Opt. Mater.">68</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and other transition metal solid solution M′<sub>1-x</sub>M<sub>x</sub>″CT<sub>z</sub> MXenes with either 20% Nb or V mixed with Ti in the form of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>Nb<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>V<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span class="figureLink bibLink tab-link"><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0012" class="figureLink bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0012_R_d167646962e2682" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A photograph of the colloidal solutions in DI water is inserted. b) XRD spectra of free-standing films created from Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, the appearance of the films, and their cross section observed by SEM are inserted. c) XPS spectra of (I-II) Ti2p and (III-IV) W4f binding energies obtained from the surface of the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, respectively.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The XRD spectra of the free-standing Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> films (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7b</a>) confirm the basal plane alignment and M<sub>2</sub>XT<sub>z</sub> and M<sub>3</sub>X<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> 2D carbide crystal structure,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0069" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0069_R_d167646962e2749" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 69 - Chem. Mater.">69</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> respectively. Moreover, it reflects the purity of the MXene in that there are no other crystalline phases present. The XPS spectra for Ti2p and W4f for both kinds of MXenes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7c</a>) reveal the chemical state of the surface Ti and W atoms. It is evident that the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> MXene is more oxidized than Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. The oxidation is also reflected in the high brittleness of the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> film compared to Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, as shown in the SEM micrographs inserted in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0007">7b</a>. This is not surprising given the much higher oxidation susceptibility of M<sub>2</sub>XT<sub>z</sub> compared to M<sub>3</sub>X<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0070" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0070_R_d167646962e2802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 70 - Inorg. Chem.">70</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0071" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0071_R_d167646962e2802" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 71 - Angew. Chem.">71</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Furthermore, the XPS results suggest a stronger inclination of Ti over W, although it has been shown that substitution of Ti with another transition element like Ta can improve electrochemical oxidation resistance in (Ti<sub>0.95</sub>Ta<sub>0.05</sub>)<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0072" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0072_R_d167646962e2818" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 72 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">72</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0080">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0080-title">2.6 Performance of the Ti<sub>1-x</sub>-W<sub>x</sub> MXenes as SIB Anodes</h3>
            
            <p>The synthesized multilayer&nbsp;MXenes were tested as SIB anodes in two different electrolytes. For Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> in carbonate electrolyte, the measured reversible capacities are 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 55 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 50 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 44 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and 30 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@1000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) (<b>Figure</b> <a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8a</a>; Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a>, Supporting Information). When the same MXene was tested in diglyme (DG) electrolyte, the capacity at lower currents was comparable, while&nbsp;it was higher (40 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@1000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>)) compared to the ones tested in carbonate electrolyte at higher currents (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8b</a>). This MXene shows a remarkable rate performance where nearly ≈50–60% of the capacity is retained even when the current is increased twentyfold. Moreover, the capacity at lower currents is completely recovered even after subjecting the MXene to high currents as seen in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8a</a>, where the capacity at 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> current is the same before and after the rate performance measurements.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adfm202406499-fig-0008"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5e8798a5-3414-43c6-860f-8f1a2b4cca22/adfm202406499-fig-0008-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/5e8798a5-3414-43c6-860f-8f1a2b4cca22/adfm202406499-fig-0008-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/65ea3da3-5be4-4aac-9637-a8912293c14d/adfm202406499-fig-0008-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5e8798a5-3414-43c6-860f-8f1a2b4cca22/adfm202406499-fig-0008-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 8<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adfm202406499-fig-0008&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadfm.202406499" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Cycling and Rate Performance of (Ti<sub>,</sub>W)<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> in (a) carbonate (EC:DMC:FEC) and b) glyme electrolyte (DG), and of (Ti<sub>,</sub>W)<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> in (c) carbonate and d) glyme electrolyte. A zoom-in view of each graph separating the cycling data from rate performance is plotted in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information) for ease of visualization.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The results are even more impressive in the case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> MXene in carbonate electrolyte and the values are approximately double compared to the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. The capacity measured was 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 105 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@ 100&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 95 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), 83 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>), and 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (@1000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>) (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8c</a>). Similar values were obtained for the same MXene tested in DG-based electrolytes as well (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8d</a>). It is appreciable that the capacity value of 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> obtained at a high current of 1000&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in the case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> is still higher than the capacity of 60 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> obtained at a small current of 50&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. In the rate performance measurements of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>, ≈60% of the capacity is also maintained when the current is increased twentyfold. In the case of carbonate electrolyte, the first cycle capacity is only ≈100 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, which is significantly lower than other MXenes. When comparing the capacity of various multilayered MXene Na-ion anodes in literature we find that the performance of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> is the best when comparing factors like capacity, rate performance, and first cycle capacity loss (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>The impact of the electrolytes on Coulombic efficiency (CE) is significant. With carbonate electrolytes, the CEs approach 100% after the initial cycles and remain stable thereafter. In contrast, when using DG electrolytes, the CE consistently decreases over cycles in both types of MXenes, with the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> presenting higher stability than the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub>. This observation highlights a notable difference from graphite, where DG electrolytes typically outperform carbonate electrolytes. However, in the case of MXenes serving as electrodes for SIBs, carbonate electrolytes demonstrate superior performance.</p>
            
            <p>The dQ/dV versus voltage (V), plotted using charge-discharge curves, are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S19</a> (Supporting Information). These plots provide information about the Na intercalation/deintercalation between the MXene layers. In the dQ/dV plots, desodiation and sodiation peaks are measured at 2.6, 0.8, and 0.06&nbsp;V irrespective of the MXene type or the electrolyte. This implies that the Na ion storage mechanism is similar in all four cases. The 2.6&nbsp;V pair of peaks are the highest voltage redox reactions recorded in MXenes as per our knowledge. The exact redox reactions occurring ≈0.8 and 2.6&nbsp;V will need to be studied in detail using in-situ XRD and X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS). The low voltage peak ≈0&nbsp;V is ascribed to the Na ion storage in the carbon black used to make the battery electrodes and not related to MXene.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0090">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0090-title">2.7 Cyclic Voltammetry and Electrochemical Impedance Spectroscopy</h3>
            
            <p>To investigate Na-ion insertion−extraction behavior in (Ti,W)<sub>x</sub>C<sub>x-1</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXene electrodes, cyclic voltammetry (CV), electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS), and galvanostatic charge−discharge tests were performed (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18–S21</a>, Supporting Information). The CV curves for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXene electrodes are nominally similar in both electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a,b</a>, Supporting Information). In case of the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information) electrode, the CV curve tested in carbonate electrolyte exhibit slightly subdued redox peaks intensity compared to that of Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. During testing in glyme electrolytes, the redox peaks appear severely muted (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>For Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> electrodes in carbonate electrolyte, 3 pairs of anodic and cathodic peaks were observed at 2.7/2.3, 1.2/0.45, and 0.2/0.01&nbsp;V, respectively (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a</a>, Supporting Information). For glyme electrolyte the observed peak positions are at 2.7/2.4, 1.1/0.35, and 0.2/0.01&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20b</a>, Supporting Information). For the Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> electrodes tested in carbonate electrolytes, anodic and cathodic peaks were observed ≈2.8/2.2, 1.3/0.4, and 0.3/0.01&nbsp;V (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information). No distinguishable peaks are observed in the case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> electrodes tested in glyme electrolytes (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information). Wang et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0073" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0073_R_d167646962e3126" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 73 - Nat. Commun.">73</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and Kajiyama et&nbsp;al.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0023_R_d167646962e3132" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - ACS Nano">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> also observed a pair of peaks ≈2&nbsp;V when testing Ti<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> anodes in SIBs. They ascribed these peaks to intercalation Na<sup>+</sup> cations between the MXene sheets. Similarly, in our electrodes, the peaks ≈2.8/2.4&nbsp;V during charging and discharging are assumed to be due to intercalation and deintercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> cations between the layers. However, it should be noted that in our case the observed potential of intercalation/deintercalation increases significantly (≈0.5&nbsp;V) compared to the just Ti-containing MXenes. In fact, this is one of the highest reported intercalation potential reported in MXenes, comparable only to those of V<sub>2</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0074" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0074_R_d167646962e3157" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 74 - J. Phys. Chem. Lett.">74</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Therefore, we believe (Ti,W)<sub>x</sub>C<sub>x-1</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes can potentially be also used as cathodes in fast-charging SIBs. A pair of redox peaks ≈1.2/0.45&nbsp;V can be ascribed to the pseudocapacitive/surface redox charge transfer process on the surface of MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0075" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0075_R_d167646962e3168" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 75 - Adv. Mater.">75</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> While the reaction occurring in the low potential range 0–0.1&nbsp;V is ascribed to Na ion insertion−extraction in the conductive carbon additive.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0076_R_d167646962e3174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 76 - Mater. Res. Lett.">76</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
            
            <p>As seen the in charge-discharge cycling data for Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> electrodes tested in glyme electrolytes (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8d</a>), had a low coulombic efficiency of &lt;85%, indicating severe electrolyte degradation and irreversible Na ion loss in the cell which is also most probably the reason behind the featureless CV (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21b</a>, Supporting Information) that is observed compared to the other CV plots (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20a,b</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21a</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>EIS spectra were taken at different potentials during the CV tests (Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S20</a> and <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21c–j</a>, Supporting Information). Note that we did not attempt to fit the equivalent circuits for the EIS spectra due to the non-trivial nature of the complex electrode intercalation/deintercalation processes and limited understanding of the charge storage mechanisms in MXenes. Nevertheless, a common feature in the (Ti,W)<sub>x</sub>C<sub>x-1</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes is the&nbsp;semi-circular regions observed in the Nyquist plots when tested in carbonate electrolyte. In contrast it is absent or significantly smaller when testing in glyme electrolyte, especially in case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> electrodes. Furthermore, inductive loops are observed in almost all the EIS spectra of MXene cells tested in glyme electrolyte. It has been shown previously that inductive loops appear in EIS for anodes where the solid electrolyte interface (SEI) films are not stable.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0077" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0077_R_d167646962e3222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 77 - Electrochem. Solid-State Lett.">77</a>-<a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0079" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0079_R_d167646962e3222" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 79 - Electrochim. Acta">79</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This observation fits well in our case as well. In the cells tested with glyme electrolytes, the CE numbers are always lower than their carbonate electrolyte counterparts, implicating unstable SEI as well (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adfm202406499-fig-0008">8</a>). Furthermore, given the fact that the semi-circular regions in the EIS spectra are representative of charge transfer resistance (R<sub>ct</sub>) in cells. A stable SEI, which is insulating in nature, will always have higher R<sub>ct</sub> compared to cells with unstable or no SEI, as the electrolyte in those cells comes in direct contact with the electrodes every cycle.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0080" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0080_R_d167646962e3235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 80">80</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0081" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0081_R_d167646962e3235" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 81 - Nat. Commun.">81</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As discussed above, given that the CE and R<sub>ct</sub> values of glyme-based cells is lower than that of cells using carbonate electrolytes may indicate improper SEI formation. Based on the CE and the R<sub>ct</sub> values of the two electrodes tested in glyme electrolyte, it seems that SEI formation is worse for Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> compared to Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0100">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adfm202406499-sec-0100-title">2.8 Postmortem SIB Anode Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>Post-cycling SEM and XRD analysis was conducted on the electrodes to physically analyze possible MXene degradation. Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22a,e</a> (Supporting Information) shows the as-synthesized multilayer Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> powders before they undergo&nbsp;electrode preparation and subsequent electrochemical testing. Both MXenes, show similar morphology, where layers are stacked in accordion-like morphology that is characteristic of all multilayer MXenes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0082" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0082_R_d167646962e3287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 82 - Adv. Mater.">82</a>, <a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0083" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0083_R_d167646962e3287" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 83 - ACS Nano">83</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> After the electrodes of MXenes were made into cells, they were cycled for 200 cycles before opening. The SEM micrographs of cycled cells in different electrolytes are shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22b,c,f,g</a> (Supporting Information). In both the MXenes tested in glyme and carbonate electrolytes, the multilayer morphology is maintained and no significant change in the MXenes structure is observed. We note in passing that in the electrodes tested in glyme electrolytes abundant needles emerged, which are most likely comprised of electrolyte byproducts.</p>
            
            <p>XRD analysis of the pristine multilayer MXene and post-cycling electrodes was performed. In the case of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> multilayers a major peak was observed at 11.6° 2θ&nbsp;(Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, grey, bottom) which corresponds to an interlayer d-spacing of 7.6 Å that is ascribed to the basal 002 plane. A small shoulder detected at a lower angle at 9° (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, grey, bottom) is attributed to slight hydration of the layers. Note that according to XRD Rietveld analysis there may be up to 20 wt% of unetched MAX phase in the Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXene. This could mean a possible&nbsp;increase of up to 20% in true gravimetric capacity of Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, which still falls short compared to that achieved by Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> (≈75 and 112 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively). After cycling in carbonate electrolyte, the 002 peak shifts to 8.4° 2θ (d-spacing ≈10.5 Å) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, purple, second from bottom). This ≈3 Å increase in d-spacing is assigned to the intercalation of Na<sup>+</sup> ion solvated by carbonate molecules.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0076" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0076_R_d167646962e3342" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 76 - Mater. Res. Lett.">76</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A similar increase was also observed in prior work on testing of Ti<sub>3</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes in carbonate electrolytes as anodes for SIBs. In case of cells tested in glyme electrolyte the increase in d-spacing was even higher and the 002 peak was observed at 6.5° 2θ (d ≈ 13.4 Å) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, yellow, third from bottom), showing that the solvation of Na<sup>+</sup> ions is larger in glyme electrolytes compared to carbonate electrolytes. A similar trend was observed in Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> MXenes, where the 002 peak in pristine powders is at 8.5° 2θ (d ≈10.4 Å) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, green, third from top) and shifts to 6.7° 2θ (d ≈ 13 Å) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, red, second from top) and 6.5° 2θ (d ≈ 13.6 Å) (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a>, Supporting Information, red, second from top) when testing in carbonate and glyme electrolyte respectively. However, the difference in d-spacing of ≈2.9 Å in the case of both glyme and carbonate is higher for Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> compared to only 0.6 Å for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. Implying a different solvation shell structure of Na ions during intercalation between the MXene layers.</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0110">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406499-sec-0110-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>New Ti<sub>1-x</sub>-W<sub>x</sub> double transition metal solid solution MAX phases were synthesized, namely 211-type Ti<sub>2-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>AlC and 312-type Ti<sub>3-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub>, with up to 40%&nbsp;W substituting for Ti. Detailed crystallographic analysis revealed that compositions with 10–20% W produced highly pure targeted phases, while those with 30–40% W resulted in surplus phases including other MAX phases, metal carbides, intermetallics, and residual metals. Furthermore, the 312-structure exhibited a pronounced tendency to form o-MAX phases as W content increased. This distinct ordering is primarily attributed to the unique electronic structure and atomic radius of tungsten, factors that are crucial for the preferential occupation of the outer planes of the 312-type MAX phase. The Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> phases, containing 20%&nbsp;W, served as precursors for synthesizing new W-containing solid solution MXenes in the forms of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, both as multilayers and single flakes. These MXenes' optical, structural, and chemical properties were thoroughly assessed. Electrochemical testing of these MXenes as SIB anodes revealed that Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> performed significantly better than Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, achieving specific capacities of 112 and 70 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at current densities of 50 and 1000 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively, compared to 60 and 30 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> for Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>. This performance is among the highest reported for any multilayer MXene tested as SIB anodes, underscoring the potential of these materials in advanced energy storage applications.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0120">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adfm202406499-sec-0120-title">4 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0130">
            
            <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0140">
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0150">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202406499-sec-0150-title"> MAX Phases Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The starting powders used to synthesize the MAX phases were Ti (99.5%, ≈45&nbsp;µm, Eckart), W (99.9%, 1–5&nbsp;µm, Alfa Aesar), Al (99.5%, ≈325 mesh, Thermo Scientific), TiC (99.5%, ≈2&nbsp;µm, Thermo Scientific), and C (99%, ≈325 mesh, Thermo Scientific). The powder mixtures were prepared in a tumbler mixer according to molar ratios to obtain targeted (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x)2</sub>AlC and (Ti<sub>1-x</sub>W<sub>x</sub>)<sub>3</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> MAX phases, where x = 0, 0.1, 0.2, 0.3, and 0.4. For example, to yield solid solutions of Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC (211) and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> (312) powders of Ti:W:Al:TiC were mixed at ratios of 0.6:0.4:1.05:1 and 0.4:0.6:1.05:2, respectively. In all samples Al was added in 5% excess of the desired stoichiometry. The detailed powder mixture compositions and targeted stoichiometries can be found in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). 5&nbsp;g of each powder mixture were cold compacted into circular pellets with a diameter of 20&nbsp;mm under uniaxial pressure of ≈45&nbsp;MPa. The resulting green bodies were placed in an alumina crucible and inserted into an 80&nbsp;mm tube furnace (TMAX-Threefurnace2). The high-temperature synthesis was carried out at 1450 °C for 3&nbsp;h under a protective atmosphere using Ar/5%H<sub>2</sub> at a flow rate of 100 sccm. The heating and cooling rate was 5 °C min<sup>−1</sup>. After synthesis the samples were drilled and ground to a powder. The MAX phases used as MXene precursors were sieved to obtain powders with a particle size ≤20&nbsp;µm.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0160">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202406499-sec-0160-title"> MXene Synthesis</h4>
                  
                  <p>The produced Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>AlC and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>AlC<sub>2</sub> were selected as precursors for MXene synthesis. The corresponding Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub> were synthesized by selectively etching the Al layer from the MAX phase precursors. For each, 1&nbsp;g of MAX phase powder was etched in a 20&nbsp;ml mixture of HCl, HF, and DI water for 72&nbsp;h at 45 °C; using a ratio of 12:4:4 and for 12:2:6 for Ti<sub>1.6</sub>W<sub>0.4</sub>CT<sub>z</sub> and Ti<sub>2.4</sub>W<sub>0.6</sub>C<sub>2</sub>T<sub>z</sub>, respectively. The etched MAX powders were centrifuged at 3500 RPM in DI water until the pH reached 6. It was then mixed with a 20&nbsp;ml 5 wt% tetramethylammonium hydroxide (TMAOH; 98%, Thermo Scientific) solution and stirred for 3&nbsp;h with N<sub>2</sub> bubbling. The intercalated MXene mixture was centrifuged again with ethanol and DI water several times, then sonicated for 1&nbsp;h and centrifuged for 1&nbsp;h to separate the MXene colloidal solution from the multilayer sediment. Free-standing films were prepared by pouring 5&nbsp;ml of each MXene colloidal solution into a vacuum filtering system equipped with a Celgard 3105 separator.</p>
                  </section>
               
               <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adfm202406499-sec-0170">
                  
                  <h4 class="article-section__sub-title section4" id="adfm202406499-sec-0170-title"> Characterization</h4>
                  
                  <p><i>Phase analysis</i>: XRD analysis was carried out on all the synthesized powders. Powder samples were mounted on an amorphous silicon holder for data collection at room temperature using a PANalytical AERIS X-ray diffractometer with a standard Cu <i>Kα</i> x-ray radiation source (λ = 1.5406 Å). A total of 7178 points were collected within a 2θ ranges of 7°−85° with a step size of 0.01086° and scan speed of 0.0557° s<sup>−1</sup>. Unit cell dimensions, space group assignment, data reduction and finalization were done by using the GSAS software package<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0084" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0084_R_d167646962e3588" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 84 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">84</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> (ver. 5711, released 2024). The background fitting was done using a Chebyschev function. The structures were solved in the hexagonal P6<sub>3</sub>/mmc space group (no. 194) and refined through the Rietveld refinement method using a full-matrix least-squares technique, using pseudo-Voight type peak profile functions. Graphics were done by the Olex2 software package.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adfm202406499-bib-0085" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adfm202406499-bib-0085_R_d167646962e3596" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 85 - J. Appl. Crystallogr.">85</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The final refinement cycle included the atomic, instrumental, and sample parameters.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Electron microscopy</i>: The MAX powder particles obtained for Ti–20%W were examined by HRSEM (ZEISS Gemini 300). In addition, EDS was performed on powders containing intermetallic phases to estimate their composition. Ti–20%W multilayered and single flake MXenes were drop cast on TEM copper grids of continuous (Type B) or ultra-thin carbon film on lacy carbon support, respectively. Both kinds of multilayer and single flake MXenes were examined by the same HRSEM operating at 30&nbsp;kV using a STEM detector. The cross-section of the free-standing MXene films were examined by SEM (JEOL JSM-IT200). Thin lamellae for TEM examination were prepared from MAX phase particles by a focused ion beam (FIB) (FEI Helios G4 UC). Atomic scale characterization of the MAX and MXenes, deposited on TEM grids, was carried out by high resolution STEM, using a Cs-corrected (S-CORR) probe operating at 200&nbsp;kV (Thermo Fisher Scientific Spectra 200). The images were recorded in STEM mode using a HAADF detector. EDS spectra were collected with a Super-X (Thermo Fisher Scientific) silicon drift detector (SDD). The images and the EDS spectra were processed with Thermo Fisher Scientific Velox software (version 3.10).</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Spectroscopy measurements of MXene</i>: Diluted colloidal solutions containing ≈0.05&nbsp;g ml<sup>−1</sup> single flake MXenes in DI water were prepared inside quartz cuvettes and the UV–vis–NIR absorption spectra between 200–900&nbsp;nm wavelength were measured in transmission mode using a spectrophotometer (Shimadzu UV-2600). XRD examination of the MXene free-standing films was performed using the same system described above. XPS analysis was performed on the free-standing MXene films using an ESCALAB QXi system (Thermo Scientific, USA). High resolution spectra were collected with a pass energy of 20&nbsp;eV.</p>
                  
                  <p><i>Electrochemical measurements and SIB anode performance</i>: To prepare the electrodes the MXene was mixed with carbon black (99%, Alfa Aesar) and polyvinylidene fluoride (PVDF) in a ratio of 80:10:10 respectively. Then N-Methyl-2-pyrrolidone (NMP) was added to make a slurry, which was cast on an Al foil using a doctor blade. After vacuum drying electrodes were punched out and 2032 coin cells were assembled inside an Ar filled glovebox. Celgard separators were used, and Na-ion metal was used as counter electrode. Two different electrolyte solvents were tested; diglyme (DG) and a 1:1 mixture of ethylene carbonate: dimethyl carbonate (EC:DMC) with 5% fluoroethylene carbonate (FEC) (carbonate) with 1&nbsp;<span class="smallCaps">m</span> NaPF<sub>6</sub> dissolved in it. The performance of assembled cells was tested assessed by a battery tester (Neware BTS4000). The cyclic voltammograms (CV) and electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) measurements were performed using a potentiostat (Biologic). The CV scans were done at 1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup> and EIS was done in the frequency range of 100&nbsp;kHz to 1 mHz at different potentials.</p>
                  </section>
               </section>
            </section>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">

      <section class="article-section article-section__supporting" data-suppl="/doi/suppl/10.1002/adfm.202406499?onlyLog=true">
         <div class="accordion article-accordion">
